The Rule of Not Deviating from Diraya: Example of Tranquility
Rule with example: diraya over weak riwaya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule 'adam al-'udul 'an al-diraya to resolve a conflict between a weak narration and the evidentiary ruling, and identify the mufta bihi position on tuma'nina in prayer.

Core Idea
A mufti must not abandon the ruling supported by stronger evidence (diraya) for a weak or under-analyzed narration (riwaya).

In Hanafi iftaa, not all narrations carry equal weight. When a mufti encounters conflicting transmissions from the Imams, he must weigh the evidence (dalil) that underlies each narration. This lesson presents a classic example: the status of tranquility (tuma'nina) in prayer. Although some narrations from Abu Hanifa suggest it is sunna, the requirement of the evidence points to obligation. The rule 'adam al-'udul 'an al-diraya instructs the mufti to uphold the evidentiary ruling rather than follow a weaker riwaya.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
The rule 'adam al-'udul 'an al-diraya means the mufti must prioritise the ruling derived from evidence (diraya) over a weak or anomalous transmission (riwaya).
2
Identify the conflict
In the example, there are two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa on tuma'nina: one says it is sunna, another says it is wajib. Similarly for qawma (standing) and jalsa (sitting).
3
Recognize the diraya
The author states 'مقتضى الدليل الوجوب' – the requirement of the evidence is obligation. So the evidentiary analysis (diraya) points to wujub.
4
Apply the rule
Since diraya supports wujub, the mufti should adopt the position of obligation, regardless of the narration that says sunna. The weaker riwaya is set aside.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) for fatwa is that tuma'nina is wajib, as per the strength of evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة: ما في "شرح المنية" في بحث تعديل الأركان بعد ما ذكر اختلاف الرواية عن الإمام في الطمأنينة هل هي سنة أو واجبة؟ وكذا القومة والجلسة، قال: وأنت علمت أن مقتضى الدليل الوجوب كما
Sixth: What is in Sharh al-Munya in the discussion of straightening the pillars (ta'dil al-arkan) after mentioning the difference of narration from the Imam regarding tranquility (tuma'nina) whether it is sunna or obligatory, and similarly the standing upright (qawma) and sitting (jalsa). He said: And you have known that the requirement of the evidence is obligation, as...
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This rule, though incomplete, illustrates the principle of 'adam al-'udul 'an al-diraya (not deviating from the established understanding). The example is from prayer: the issue of tranquility (tuma'nina) and the positions of standing and sitting after bowing and prostration. There is a difference of narration (ikhtilaf al-riwaya) from Imam Abu Hanifa whether these are sunna or obligatory (wajib). The author states that the requirement of the evidence (muqtada al-dalil) is obligation (wujub). This means that even if some narrations suggest sunna, the stronger evidence (diraya) points to obligation, and one should not deviate from that. The rule prohibits abandoning diraya in favor of a weak or under-analyzed riwaya.

Key Terms
الطمأنينة (al-tuma'nina): Tranquility; pausing in prayer until each joint settles.
الدراية (al-diraya): Derived understanding; the ruling reached through analysis and evidence, as opposed to mere transmission.
الرواية (al-riwaya): A transmitted report or narration, especially from the Hanafi imams.
العدول عن الدراية (al-'udul 'an al-diraya): Abandoning the evidentiary ruling in favor of a weaker narration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rule of 'adam al-'udul 'an al-diraya, how should a mufti treat the issue of tuma'nina in prayer when there are conflicting narrations?
A) Follow the narration that says it is sunna, since it is a lighter view.
B) Follow the ruling that is supported by the strength of evidence (diraya).
C) Leave the matter undecided.
D) Follow the most famous narration regardless of evidence.

Key Takeaway
When evidence (diraya) conflicts with a weak transmission (riwaya), the mufti must uphold the evidentiary ruling.

The Rule of Retraction: Rulings Outside Zahir al-Riwayah
Rasm al-mufti rule: hierarchy of narrations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify rulings outside Zahir al-Riwayah, apply the rule of retraction (marju' 'anhu), and determine when an anomalous riwaya may be used for fatwa.

Core Idea
A ruling outside Zahir al-Riwayah is retracted; it is no longer a valid opinion of the mujtahid unless later scholars explicitly adopt it.

This passage establishes a fundamental principle for muftis and judges: the primary texts of the Hanafi school are the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah. Any ruling found outside these books is considered retracted by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, meaning it does not represent the final madhhab position. However, the rule has important exceptions that a mufti must understand to correctly navigate the madhhab's textual hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary rule
The passage states: 'what is outside Zahir al-Riwaya is retracted.' Understand that 'Zahir al-Riwayah' refers to the six core works of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
Zahir al-Riwayah = al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Ziyadat.
2
Understand the consequence of retraction
A retracted ruling ceases to be a valid opinion (qawl) of the mujtahid. It cannot be used as the basis for fatwa or judgment unless an exception applies.
Analogous to naskh (abrogation) in effect.
3
Apply the rule to the muqallid judge
A muqallid judge may only judge by the apparent madhhab (Zahir al-Riwayah). He cannot use an anomalous riwaya (riwaya shadhdhah) unless later scholars explicitly state that the fatwa is upon it.
This safeguards against arbitrary rulings.
4
Identify the exception for missing rulings
If a matter is not mentioned anywhere in Zahir al-Riwayah but is established in another reliable riwaya, then it is necessary to follow that other riwaya.
Here, 'necessity' (ta'ayyana) means you must use the only available evidence.
5
Note the nuance: explicit endorsement by later scholars
Even for a shadhdh riwaya, if later Hanafi masters (e.g., al-Quduri, al-Marghinani) explicitly say 'the fatwa is upon this,' then it can be used despite being outside Zahir al-Riwayah.
This is a form of later tarjih that can revive a retracted view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة ما في قضاء "البحر": من أن ما خرج عن ظاهر الرواية، فهو مرجوع عنه. والمرجوع عنه لم يبق قولا للمجتهد كما ذكروه. وقدمنا "أنفع المسائل": أن القاضي المقلد لا يجوز له أن يحكم إلا بما هو ظاهر المذهب لا بالرواية الشاذة إلا أن ينصّوا على أن الفتوى عليها. وفي قضاء الفوائت من "البحر": "أن المسألة إذا لم تذكر في ظاهر الرواية وثبتت في رواية أخرى تعين المصير إليها".
The fifth: what is in 'Qada' al-Bahr': that whatever is outside Zahir al-Riwaya, it is retracted. And the retracted statement no longer remains a view of the mujtahid, as they mentioned. And we previously presented from 'Anfa' al-Masa'il': that a judge who is a muqallid is not permitted to judge except by what is the apparent madhhab, not by the anomalous riwaya, unless they specify that the fatwa is upon it. And in 'Qada' al-Fawait' from 'al-Bahr': 'That if a matter is not mentioned in Zahir al-Riwaya but is established in another riwaya, it is necessary to follow it.'
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage establishes a key rasm rule regarding the authority of different narrative transmissions. The 'Zahir al-Riwayah'—the six core works of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani—are the primary source of the madhhab. Any ruling that falls outside these works is considered 'marju' 'anhu' (retracted) and does not represent the mujtahid's final position. A muqallid judge or mufti is generally bound to follow only the apparent madhhab (i.e., Zahir al-Riwayah) and not anomalous (shadhdh) reports. However, an exception is noted: if the later scholars explicitly state that the fatwa is upon that anomalous riwaya, then it may be followed. Additionally, for matters not covered in Zahir al-Riwayah but found in another reliable riwaya, one must follow that other riwaya, as the rule is to apply the only available evidence.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Ziyadat.
رواية شاذة (riwaya shadhdhah): Anomalous or rare transmission that contradicts the majority narrations.
مرجوع عنه (marju' 'anhu): A ruling that the mujtahid has retracted; it no longer forms part of the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a judge permitted to use a ruling from a shadhdh riwaya (anomalous narration)?
A) Whenever the ruling appears easier for the people.
B) Only when the ruling is mentioned in a later commentary.
C) Only when later scholars explicitly state that the fatwa is upon that shadhdh riwaya.
D) Never, because shadhdh riwayat are always rejected.

Key Takeaway
The default rule is that rulings outside Zahir al-Riwayah are retracted; they regain authority only when later scholars expressly adopt them or when the matter is absent from Zahir al-Riwayah entirely.

Do Not Deviate from Dalil Supported by Riwaya
Rule: do not deviate from dalil when supported by riwaya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rasm al-mufti rule that when evidence (dalil) indicates a ruling and a riwaya supports it, the mufti must adhere to the evidence-based ruling, not abandon it for a conflicting riwaya.

Core Idea
When dalil and riwaya agree, do not turn away from the evidence.

In this passage from Sharh al-Munya, the author addresses a disagreement about the status of tuma'ninah (tranquility) in prayer – is it sunnah or wajib? He cites Shaykh Kamal al-Din stating that the evidence (dalil) indicates obligation. Then he formulates a key rule for the mufti: it is not appropriate to deviate from the evidence when a narration supports it. This lesson unpacks that rule and clarifies how a mufti should weigh evidence and transmitted reports.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ikhtilaf
Determine the disputed ruling in the mas'alah. Here, it is whether tuma'ninah is sunnah or wajib.
The passage mentions this ikhtilaf exists from Imam Abu Hanifa.
2
Determine the evidence (dalil)
Using usul principles, derive what the evidence (such as hadith) indicates. For tuma'ninah, dalil points to wujub.
Shaykh Kamal al-Din is cited as stating the evidence indicates obligation.
3
Check for supporting riwaya
See if there exists a transmitted narration (riwaya) from the Imam that supports the evidence-based conclusion.
If a riwaya agrees with the dalil, the rule applies.
4
Apply the rule
If such a riwaya exists, adhere to the evidence-based ruling; do not abandon it for a weaker conflicting narration.
The rule: 'لا ينبغي أن يعدل عن الدراية إذا وافقتها رواية' – do not deviate from evidence when a riwaya supports it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة: ما في "شرح المنية" في بحث تعديل الأركان بعد ما ذكر اختلاف الرواية عن الإمام في الطمأنينة هل هي سنة أو واجبة؟ وكذا القومة والجلسة، قال: وأنت علمت أن مقتضى الدليل الوجوب كما قاله الشيخ كمال الدين، ولا ينبغي أن يعدل عن الدراية إذا وافقتها رواية. والدراية – بالدال المهملة - تستعمل بمعنى الدليل كما في "المستصفى".
The sixth: what is in 'Sharh al-Munya' in the discussion of the adjustment of pillars after he mentioned the disagreement of narration from the Imam regarding tranquility (tuma'ninah): is it sunnah or obligatory? And similarly the standing and the sitting. He said: 'And you know that the requirement of the evidence (dalil) is obligation, as Shaykh Kamal al-Din said, and it is not appropriate to deviate from the evidence (dirayah) when a narration supports it.' And 'dirayah' – with the unpointed dal – is used with the meaning of evidence (dalil) as in 'al-Mustasfa'.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage gives a specific application of a broader rasm rule: when determining the legal status of an action in prayer (like tranquility, standing, sitting), there is a difference of narration from Imam Abu Hanifa: is it sunnah or wajib? The informing principle here is that the evidence (dalil) points to it being wajib (obligatory), as stated by Shaykh Kamal al-Din. The rule formulated is: it is not appropriate to turn away from the evidence (dirayah) when a riwaya (narrative transmission) agrees with that evidence. Notably, 'dirayah' here is explained as synonymous with 'dalil' (evidence), not mere reasoning. So if a transmitted report from Imam Abu Hanifa supports what the evidence indicates, the mufti should adhere to the evidence-based ruling, not abandon it for a conflicting riwaya that might be weaker in proof.

Key Terms
دراية (dirayah): Evidence (dalil); used interchangeably with dalil in this context.
طمأنينة (tuma'ninah): Tranquility or stillness in prayer, the state of being calm after actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does 'dirayah' mean in the context of this rule?
A) Independent reasoning without reference to texts.
B) Evidence (dalil), as used in al-Mustasfa.
C) A specific narration from Imam Abu Hanifa.
D) The opinion of Shaykh Kamal al-Din.

Key Takeaway
The mufti must not turn away from the evidence (dalil) when a transmitted narration (riwaya) supports it; evidence combined with a supporting narration takes precedence over conflicting narrations.

Prefer Non-Takfir Rulings When Possible
Rule to prefer non-takfir when there is any riwaya or interpretation to avoid takfir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a mufti must avoid takfir if any possible good interpretation exists, any riwaya (even weak) supports non-takfir, or any scholarly disagreement (ikhtilaf) is present.

Core Idea
A mufti must not declare a Muslim a kafir if there is any way to avoid it—even a weak narration suffices to prevent takfir.

The gravity of takfir demands extreme caution. This lesson examines a fundamental principle from the Hanafi rasm al-mufti: when a person's words or actions could imply kufr, the mufti must exhaust all possibilities of favorable interpretation before even considering takfir. The rule prioritizes preserving Islamic identity over punishing apparent deviation.

Guided Path
1
Examine the statement/action
Identify what the person said or did that might imply kufr.
2
Search for a good interpretation
Consider all possible favorable interpretations (mahmal hasan) of the person's words. Could it be a slip, anger, misinterpretation, etc.? The default assumption is that the person is Muslim.
3
Check for any riwaya supporting non-kufr
If no good interpretation immediately comes, look into the Hanafi corpus for any transmission (riwaya) that categorizes this statement as not kufr. Even a weak riwaya suffices.
Weak riwaya can originate from later mutakhkhir scholars.
4
Determine if there is ikhtilaf
If there is any scholarly disagreement about whether such a statement constitutes kufr (even if the dissenting view is weak), avoid takfir.
Ikhtilaf can be between schools or within the Hanafi madhhab.
5
Issue the fatwa
If any of the above conditions are met, do not issue a fatwa of takfir. Only if absolutely no interpretation or riwaya exists should one consider takfir, and even then it is makruh to do so.
The principle is to preserve the person's Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابعة ما في "البحر" من باب المرتد نقلا عن "الفتاوى الصغرى": الكفر شيء عظيم فلا أجعل المؤمن كافرا متى وجدت رواية أنه لا يكفر. ثم قال والذي تحرر أنه لا يفتى بكفر مسلم أمكن حمل كلامه على محمل حسن، أو كان في كفره اختلاف ولو رواية ضعيفة.
The seventh: what is in 'al-Bahr' from the chapter of apostate quoting from 'al-Fatawa al-Sughra': 'Kufr is a grave matter, so I do not declare a believer a disbeliever whenever I find a narration that he does not disbelieve.' Then he said: 'And what is established is that a Muslim is not given a fatwa of disbelief if his statement can be interpreted in a good way, or if there is disagreement about his disbelief even if by a weak narration.'
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage lays down a fundamental principle regarding takfir (declaring someone a disbeliever). The rule is that kufr is a serious matter; therefore, a mufti should not issue a fatwa of disbelief against a Muslim as long as there exists any riwaya (narrative transmission) that indicates the person is not a disbeliever. Moreover, even if there is disagreement about the person's disbelief—even if the conflicting opinion is based on a weak narration (riwaya da'ifah)—the mufti should avoid takfir. The established position is that one should not give a fatwa of kufr if the person's words can be interpreted in a favorable light (mahmal hasan). This reflects the Hanafi school's cautious approach to takfir, preserving the default status of Islam whenever possible.

Key Terms
تكفير (takfir): The act of declaring someone a disbeliever (kafir).
رواية ضعيفة (riwaya da'ifah): A weak or less authentic transmission from the Imams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this rasm rule, when should a mufti avoid issuing a fatwa of takfir?
A) Only if there is a strong (sahih) narration that the person is Muslim.
B) If any possible good interpretation exists or any disagreement (even with a weak narration) is present.
C) Only if the person explicitly denies the shahada.
D) If the mufti personally feels uncertain.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi madhhab, a mufti must not issue a fatwa of takfir if any good interpretation or any narration (even weak) can save the person from disbelief.

8 min
The Retracted Opinion: Like Abrogated, Must Be Left
Rule from al-Bahr and Sharh al-Tahrir on effect of mujtahid's retraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a retracted opinion of a mujtahid is like abrogated and identify the two scenarios based on knowledge of chronology.

Core Idea
A retracted opinion loses all authority; the mujtahid's later statement is his true madhhab.

A mufti often encounters conflicting rulings attributed to the same Imam. How does he know which one to follow? This lesson presents a key rule from the Hanafi rasm al-mufti: when an Imam retracts a previous opinion, that opinion ceases to be part of his madhhab and becomes like an abrogated ruling. The mufti must then seek out the new opinion. However, a nuance arises when the chronological order is unknown – in that case, both opinions are reported without declaring retraction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule from al-Bahr
Read the passage from al-Bahr: 'the retracted opinion no longer remains a madhhab of the mujtahid, and then it is obligatory to seek the opinion to which he retracted and act upon it; because the first became like an abrogated ruling.' Note that this makes retraction analogous to abrogation (naskh) in terms of authority loss.
This is a strong rule: the retracted opinion is completely out of the madhhab.
2
Understand the analogy to abrogation
The term 'mansukh' (abrogated) is used figuratively. In Sharia, an abrogated verse is no longer operative; similarly, a retracted opinion loses all legal force. The mufti cannot use it for fatwa.
This is juristic retraction, not textual naskh, but the practical effect is the same.
3
Distinguish two scenarios from Sharh al-Tahrir
Sharh al-Tahrir adds: if the later opinion is known, it is the madhhab and the first is abrogated. If chronology is unknown, both opinions are simply narrated without judging one as retracted.
When chronology is unknown, the mufti must not assume retraction; he reports both and may need other tarjih principles.
4
Apply to mufti practice
In practice, a mufti who finds two contradictory statements from an Imam must first attempt to determine which came later. If later is known, he must follow it and discard the earlier. If not, he cites both opinions and uses other criteria (e.g., preponderance, wide acceptance) to choose.
This is the operative rule for fatwa: the later known opinion is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامنة: ما في "البحر" مما قدمناه قريبا: "من أن المرجوع عنه لم يبق مذهبا للمجتهد، وحينئذ فيجب طلب القول الذي رجع إليه والعمل به؛ لأن الأول صار بمنزلة الحكم "المنسوخ ". وفي "البحر" أيضا عن "التوشيح": أن ما رجع عنه المجتهد لا يجوز الأخذ به". وذكر في "شرح التحرير" : "إن علم المتأخر فهو مذهبه، ويكون الأول منسوخا، وإلا حكي عنه القولان من غير أن يحكم على أحدهما بالرجوع".
The eighth: what is in 'al-Bahr' from what we presented recently: 'That the retracted opinion no longer remains a madhhab of the mujtahid, and then it is obligatory to seek the opinion to which he retracted and act upon it; because the first became like an abrogated ruling.' And in 'al-Bahr' also from 'al-Tawshih': 'That what the mujtahid retracted from is not permissible to take.' And it is mentioned in 'Sharh al-Tahrir': 'If the later [opinion] is known, it is his madhhab, and the first is abrogated; otherwise, both opinions are narrated from him without judging one as retracted.'
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents a crucial rasm rule regarding the effect of a mujtahid's retraction (ruju'). Once a mujtahid (e.g., Imam Abu Hanifa or his disciples) retracts a previous opinion, that opinion ceases to be part of his madhhab. As a result, it becomes obligatory (wajib) for the mufti to seek out the new opinion (marju' ilayh) and act upon it, because the old opinion is effectively abrogated (mansukh). This is supported by a quote from al-Tawshih stating it is not permissible (la yajuz) to take the retracted opinion. However, there is a nuance from Sharh al-Tahrir: if it is known which opinion came later, that later opinion is his madhhab and the first is abrogated. If the chronological order is unknown, then both opinions are simply reported without declaring one as retracted. In that case, the mufti would need other tarjih principles to choose.

Key Terms
مرجوع عنه (marju' 'anhu): The opinion that has been retracted by the mujtahid.
منسوخ (mansukh): Abrogated; used analogously here to indicate that the earlier opinion is no longer operative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must a mufti do when it is known that an Imam retracted an earlier opinion?
A) Choose whichever opinion seems stronger to him.
B) Seek and act upon the opinion to which the Imam retracted.
C) Continue using the earlier opinion because it is from a mujtahid.
D) Discard both opinions and look elsewhere.

Key Takeaway
When a mujtahid's later opinion is known, the retracted opinion is like abrogated and must not be used; if chronology unknown, both opinions are reported without retraction judgment.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
Obligation to Adopt the Retracted Opinion
Ruling on adopting the opinion a mujtahid retracted to

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a mujtahid's retracted opinion is no longer valid for fatwa, and identify which opinion is mufta bihi when narrations differ.

Core Idea
A mujtahid's retracted opinion is like an abrogated ruling—only the opinion he retracted to is valid for fatwa.

A mufti may encounter conflicting narrations from a single mujtahid, such as Imam Abu Hanifa. This passage establishes a critical rasm rule: the opinion that the mujtahid retracted to (al-marjū‘ ilayhi) becomes his final madhhab, and earlier views become like abrogated rulings. Understanding this hierarchy prevents reliance on outdated positions and ensures fatwas align with the mujtahid's final ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
Determine which of the mujtahid's opinions is earlier and which is later. The later opinion is the one he retracted to.
If the chronology is unknown, both are reported without judging retraction.
2
Check if the later opinion is known
If the later opinion is reliably known, it becomes the madhhab; the earlier is treated as mansukh (abrogated).
This is taken from Sharh al-Tahrir.
3
Apply the evidence priority rule
When narrations differ but chronology is unclear, adopt the one with the strongest evidence (hujja). Al-Hawi al-Qudsi states: 'The most preferable is the strongest in evidence.'
Even if a narration is weak, it may be preferred if it avoids a serious ruling like takfir.
4
Consider the gravity of the ruling
In matters of takfir, prefer the narration that avoids kufr, even if weak, because kufr is grave and caution is required.
Al-Bahr quotes: 'I do not make a believer a kafir whenever I find a narration that he is not a kafir.'
5
Conclude the mufta bihi
Based on the above, determine the operative opinion for fatwa: the later opinion if known, or the strongest evidence, with preference for leniency in serious matters.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school.

قاله الشيخ كمال الدين، ولا ينبغي أن يعدل عن الدراية إذا وافقتها رواية". والدراية

الثامنة: ما في "البحر" مما قدمناه قريبا: "من أن المرجوع عنه لم يبق مذهبا للمجتهد، وحينئذ فيجب طلب القول الذي رجع إليه والعمل به؛ لأن الأول صار بمنزلة الحكم "المنسوخ ". وفي "البحر" أيضا عن
"التوشيح": أن ما رجع عنه المجتهد لا يجوز الأخذ به".
وذكر في "شرح التحرير" : "إن علم المتأخر فهو مذهبه، ويكون الأول منسوخا، وإلا حكي عنه القولان من غير أن يحكم على أحدهما بالرجوع"..
It is necessary to adopt the opinion retracted to. Said by Shaykh Kamal al-Din: It is not appropriate to deviate from the proof (diraya) if it agrees with a narration. And "diraya" with an unpointed dal is used to mean evidence (dalil) as in al-Mustasfa. This is supported by what is in the end of al-Hawi al-Qudsi: "When narrations from Abu Hanifa differ on a matter, the most preferable to adopt is the strongest in evidence." The narration of non-takfir is preponderant. Seventh: What is in al-Bahr from the chapter of apostate, quoting from al-Fatawa al-Sughra: Kufr is a grave matter, so I do not make a believer a kafir whenever I find a narration that he is not a kafir. Then he said: What is determined is that a Muslim is not given a fatwa of kufr if it is possible to interpret his words in a good way, or if there is a difference of opinion regarding his kufr, even if the narration is weak. It is necessary to adopt the opinion retracted to. Eighth: What is in al-Bahr which we recently mentioned: "That the retracted opinion is no longer the madhhab of the mujtahid, and then it is obligatory to seek the opinion he retracted to and act upon it; because the first becomes like an abrogated ruling." And in al-Bahr also from al-Tawshih: "That what the mujtahid has retracted from, it is not permissible to act upon it." And it is mentioned in Sharh al-Tahrir: "If the later opinion is known, it is his madhhab, and the first is abrogated; otherwise, both opinions are narrated without judging one as retracted."
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents a rasm rule for muftis: when a mujtahid (like Abu Hanifa) retracts an earlier opinion and adopts a later one, it is obligatory to adopt the later opinion. The retracted opinion has no remaining authority, similar to an abrogated ruling. The passage supports this with the principle that when narrations from Abu Hanifa differ, the strongest in evidence (hujja) is taken. It also gives an example from apostasy: even a weak narration that avoids takfir should be preferred, because takfir is grave. The rule is further explained: if the later opinion is known, it becomes the madhhab; if not, both are reported without assigning retraction.

Key Terms
المرجوع إليه (al-marjū‘ ilayhi): The opinion that the mujtahid retracted to and adopted as his final view.
المرجوع عنه (al-marjū‘ ‘anhu): The earlier opinion that the mujtahid abandoned.
الدراية (al-dirāyah): Used here to mean evidence (dalil) as per al-Mustasfa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The opinion that is easier for the people
B) The opinion that has the strongest evidence (hujja)
C) The most widely narrated opinion
D) The opinion of the later Hanafi scholars

Key Takeaway
When a mujtahid retracts an opinion, his earlier view is like an abrogated ruling and cannot be relied upon for fatwa.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Hierarchy of Hanafi Fatwa Sources: Matn, Sharh, Fatawa
Hierarchy of Hanafi textual authority for fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of precedence (matn, sharh, fatawa) when resolving conflicting Hanafi texts and distinguish between implicit and explicit correction (tasih iltizami vs tasih sarih).

Core Idea
Matn rulings are primary for fatwa; explicit correction in later works overrides implicit correction in matn.

When a mufti encounters conflicting rulings across Hanafi texts, he must know which source to prioritize. This lesson introduces the classical hierarchy: matn (core texts), then sharh (commentaries), then fatawa (fatwa collections). You will learn why matn are given precedence and when a later explicit correction can override them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three text types in the hierarchy
List matn, sharh, and fatawa as the three levels of authority. Matn are the foundational texts, sharh are commentaries, and fatawa are later compilations.
2
Understand the basis: tasih iltizami
Matn authors implicitly commit to recording the correct and relied-upon opinion. This implicit correction (tasih iltizami) gives matn its default authority.
3
Recognize the exception: tasih sarih
Explicit correction (tasih sarih) in a sharh or fatawa work overrides the matn's implicit correction because it is a direct statement of correctness.
4
Apply the hierarchy to a conflict
When a later fatwa contradicts a matn, check if the fatwa explicitly corrects the matn. If yes, follow the fatwa; if not, the matn prevails. The same logic applies between sharh and fatawa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقدم المتون ثم الشروح ثم الفتاوى
يقدّم المتون ثم الشروح ثم الفتاوى]
التاسعة ما ذكره العلامة قاسم في "تصحيحه ": "أن ما في المتون مصحح تصحيحا التزاميا، والتصحيح الصريح مقدم على التصحيح
الالتزامي".
قلت حاصله أن أصحاب المتون التزموا وضع القول الصحيح، فيكون ما في غيرها مقابل الصحيح، ما لم يصرح بتصحيحه، فيقدم عليها؛ لأنه تصحيح صريح، فيقدم على التصحيح الالتزامي. وفي شهادات "الخيرية" في جواب سؤال: المذهب الصحيح المفتى به الذي مشت عليه أصحاب المتون الموضوعة لنقل الصحيح من المذهب الذي هو ظاهر الرواية أن شهادة الأعمى لا تصح". ثم قال: وحيث علم أن القول هو الذي تواردت عليه المتون فهو المعتمد المعمول به؛ إذ صرحوا بأنه إذا تعارض ما في المتون والفتاوى، فالمعتمد ما في وكذا يقدم ما في الشروح على ما في الفتاوى". وفي فصل الحبس من "البحر": "والعمل على ما في المتون؛ لأنه إذا تعارض ما في المتون والفتاوى فالمعتمد ما في المتون، كما في "أنفع الوسائل"، وكذا يقدم ما في الشروح على ما في الفتاوى". أي لما صرح به في "أنفع الوسائل" أيضا في مسألة قسمة الوقف حيث قال: "لا يفتى بنقول الفتاوى، بل نقول الفتاوى إنما يستأنس بها إذا لم يوجد ما يعارضها من كتب الأصول ونقل المذهب، أما مع وجود غيرها
المتون،
لا يلتفت إليها خصوصا إذا لم يكن نص فيها على الفتوى".
Matn (core texts) are given precedence, then sharh (commentaries), then fatawa (fatwa collections). Ninth: What Allamah Qasim mentioned in his Tashih: "That what is in the matn is considered corrected by implicit correction (tasih iltizami), and explicit correction takes precedence over implicit correction." I said: The gist is that authors of matn committed themselves to recording the correct opinion, so what is in other works is in contrast to the correct, unless it is explicitly corrected, then it takes precedence over them; because it is explicit correction, so it takes precedence over implicit correction. And in the Shahadat section of al-Khayriyya in answer to a question: The correct madhhab for fatwa, which the authors of matn composed for transmitting the correct from the madhhab that is zahir al-riwaya, is that the testimony of a blind person is invalid. Then he said: When it is known that an opinion is agreed upon in the matn, it is the relied-upon and acted-upon; because they stated that when what is in the matn and fatawa conflict, the relied-upon is what is in the matn. And similarly, what is in the sharh is given precedence over what is in the fatawa. And in the chapter on habs from al-Bahr: "Act upon what is in the matn; because when what is in the matn and fatawa conflict, the relied-upon is what is in the matn, as in Anfa' al-Wasa'il. And similarly, what is in the sharh is given precedence over what is in the fatawa." That is, because it is stated in Anfa' al-Wasa'il also, in the issue of division of waqf, where he said: "Fatwa is not given based on the transmissions of fatawa books; rather, the transmissions of fatawa books are only used for support if nothing contradicts them from the books of usul and transmitted madhhab. But in the presence of other matn, they are not heeded, especially if there is no explicit fatwa in them."
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage establishes a hierarchy of textual authority for Hanafi muftis: core matn (like al-Mukhtasar, al-Quduri) are first priority, then commentaries (shuruh), then fatwa collections (fatawa). The reasoning is that matn authors implicitly commit to recording the correct and relied-upon opinion (tasih iltizami). However, if another work contains an explicit correction (tasih sarih), that explicit correction overrides the implicit one. The passage provides examples: the testimony of a blind person is invalid according to the matn, and that is the relied-upon ruling despite what fatawa may say. Similarly, in waqf division, fatawa are only used for reinforcement, not primary authority. This rule prevents confusion when sources appear to conflict.

Key Terms
المتون (al-mutūn): Core Hanafi texts (e.g., al-Quduri, al-Mukhtasar) that compile the relied-upon rulings.
الشروح (al-shurūḥ): Commentaries on the matn, providing explanation and occasionally alternative views.
الفتاوى (al-fatāwā): Later fatwa collections that may include weaker or adapted rulings.
تصحيح التزامي (taṣḥīḥ iltizāmī): Implicit correction by virtue of being in a matn, presumed correct because the author committed to recording the authentic opinion.
تصحيح صريح (taṣḥīḥ ṣarīḥ): Explicit correction in a commentary or fatwa book, directly stating an opinion is correct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the order of precedence when texts conflict?
A) Fatawa, then matn, then sharh
B) Matn, then sharh, then fatawa
C) Sharh, then matn, then fatawa
D) Fatawa, then sharh, then matn

Key Takeaway
When Hanafi texts conflict, prioritize matn, then sharh, then fatawa, unless an explicit correction in a later source overrides.

The Ninth Rule: Explicit vs Implicit Correction in Hanafi Fatwa
Establishes the ninth rule: hierarchy of corrections and textual authorities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that explicit correction (tas'hih sarih) takes precedence over implicit correction (tas'hih iltizami) in Hanafi fatwa methodology, and correctly identify the hierarchy among mutun, shuruh, and fatawa.

Core Idea
Explicit correction overrides implicit correction, but the mutun remain the primary source of the madhhab.

This lesson introduces the ninth rule of Rasm al-Mufti, which governs how a mufti should prioritize textual authorities when issuing fatwas. You will learn the distinction between implicit correction in the authoritative matn texts and explicit corrections found in commentary or fatwa works. The passage from 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti establishes a clear hierarchy: mutun are primary, followed by shuruh, then fatawa, but explicit correction can tip the scales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
Read the opening statement: Allamah Qasim's rule that explicit correction takes precedence over implicit correction. Understand that the mutun contain implicit correction (iltizami), meaning the author committed to including only the correct ruling.
2
Distinguish implicit vs explicit
Implicit correction (tas'hih iltizami) is the default in mutun: the mere presence of a ruling implies it is correct. Explicit correction (tas'hih sarih) is a direct statement by a later scholar that a particular ruling is correct, found in shuruh or fatawa.
3
Analyze the hierarchy
The passage then clarifies the hierarchy: when mutun and fatawa conflict, the mutun prevail. Similarly, shuruh are preferred over fatawa. This is supported by citations from Al-Khayriyya, Al-Bahr, and Anfa' al-Wasa'il.
Note that fatawa are only used for guidance when no conflicting text exists in the primary sources.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
Consider a scenario: The matn states a ruling implicitly, but a later commentary explicitly corrects it. The explicit correction in the commentary should be followed. But if the matn itself explicitly states a ruling, that overrides any later explicit correction.
This is because the mutun are the foundational texts.
5
Summarize the practical implication for a mufti
A mufti must first check the mutun. If a ruling is present there, it is reliable. If a later work explicitly corrects it, that correction is adopted, provided the matn's correction was only implicit. The rule ensures consistency with the established madhhab.
The mufti should also be aware that fatawa are lower in authority and only used when primary sources are silent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسعة ما ذكره العلامة قاسم في "تصحيحه ": "أن ما في المتون مصحح تصحيحا التزاميا، والتصحيح الصريح مقدم على التصحيح الالتزامي". قلت حاصله أن أصحاب المتون التزموا وضع القول الصحيح، فيكون ما في غيرها مقابل الصحيح، ما لم يصرح بتصحيحه، فيقدم عليها؛ لأنه تصحيح صريح، فيقدم على التصحيح الالتزامي. وفي شهادات "الخيرية" في جواب سؤال: المذهب الصحيح المفتى به الذي مشت عليه أصحاب المتون الموضوعة لنقل الصحيح من المذهب الذي هو ظاهر الرواية أن شهادة الأعمى لا تصح". ثم قال: وحيث علم أن القول هو الذي تواردت عليه المتون فهو المعتمد المعمول به؛ إذ صرحوا بأنه إذا تعارض ما في المتون والفتاوى، فالمعتمد ما في المتون. وكذا يقدم ما في الشروح على ما في الفتاوى". وفي فصل الحبس من "البحر": "والعمل على ما في المتون؛ لأنه إذا تعارض ما في المتون والفتاوى فالمعتمد ما في المتون، كما في "أنفع الوسائل"، وكذا يقدم ما في الشروح على ما في الفتاوى". أي لما صرح به في "أنفع الوسائل" أيضا في مسألة قسمة الوقف حيث قال: "لا يفتى بنقول الفتاوى، بل نقول الفتاوى إنما يستأنس بها إذا لم يوجد ما يعارضها من كتب الأصول ونقل المذهب، أما مع وجود غيرها لا يلتفت إليها خصوصا إذا لم يكن نص فيها على الفتوى".
Ninth: What Allamah Qasim mentioned in his 'Tas'hih': 'What is in the mutun is corrected with an implicit correction, and explicit correction takes precedence over implicit correction.' I say its summary is that the authors of the mutun committed themselves to placing the correct statement, so what is in other works is opposite the correct, unless its correction is explicitly stated, then it is preferred because it is an explicit correction, so it takes precedence over implicit correction. And in the testimony section of 'Al-Khayriyya' in answer to a question: The correct and mufta bihi madhhab upon which the authors of the mutun, which were composed to transmit the correct from the madhhab that is zahir al-riwaya, proceed, is that the testimony of a blind person is not valid. Then he said: And when it is known that the statement is one that the mutun unanimously agree upon, then it is the relied-upon and practiced-upon; for they explicitly stated that if what is in the mutun and what is in the fatawa conflict, the relied-upon is what is in the mutun. Likewise, what is in the shuruh is given precedence over what is in the fatawa. And in the chapter on habs from 'Al-Bahr': 'Practice is upon what is in the mutun; because if what is in the mutun and fatawa conflict, the relied-upon is what is in the mutun, as in 'Anfa' al-Wasa'il', and likewise what is in the shuruh is preferred over what is in the fatawa.' That is due to what was stated in 'Anfa' al-Wasa'il' also concerning the issue of division of waqf, where he said: 'Fatwa is not given based on the transmissions of fatawa; rather the transmissions of fatawa are only used for guidance when nothing conflicting is found from the books of usul and transmission of the madhhab. However, if other sources exist, they are not to be considered, especially if there is no explicit text in them regarding the fatwa.'
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary on Uqud Rasm al-Mufti, presents the ninth rule concerning the hierarchy of textual authorities in Hanafi fatwa. The author begins by quoting Allamah Qasim's 'Tas'hih', which distinguishes between two types of correction: implicit correction (tas'hih iltizami), which is the default in the mutun (core authoritative texts like al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, etc.), and explicit correction (tas'hih sarih), which is when a later scholar explicitly declares a particular ruling as correct. The rule states that explicit correction takes precedence over implicit correction. The author explains that the authors of the mutun committed themselves to including only the correct ruling, so if a ruling appears outside these mutun (e.g., in fatawa works) without explicit correction, it is considered opposite to the correct one. But if an explicit correction is found elsewhere, it overrides the implicit correction in the matn. Then the passage cites the work 'Al-Khayriyya' to affirm that the mutun are the primary source for the madhhab, and in case of conflict between mutun and fatawa, the mutun prevail. Likewise, shuruh (commentaries) are preferred over fatawa. The passage also quotes from 'Al-Bahr' and 'Anfa' al-Wasa'il', reinforcing that fatawa are only consulted when no contrary text exists in the mutun or shuruh. This rule ensures that fatwas are based on the most authoritative sources of the madhhab, preventing reliance on later opinions that may contradict the established transmission.

Key Terms
تصحيح التزامي (tas'hih iltizami): A correction that is implicit, meaning the author of a matn commits to including only the correct ruling, so the mere presence of a ruling in a matn implies it is correct.
تصحيح صريح (tas'hih sarih): An explicit correction, where a scholar states directly that a particular ruling is correct, usually in a commentary or fatwa work.
متون (mutun): The core authoritative texts of the Hanafi school, such as al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, al-Wiqaya, etc., which are composed to transmit the madhhab from the zahir al-riwaya.
فتاوى (fatawa): Works containing fatwas of later Hanafi scholars, often compiled from rulings of individual muftis. They have lower authority than mutun and shuruh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when there is a conflict between a ruling in a matn and a ruling in a fatawa collection, which is to be given precedence?
A) The ruling in the fatawa collection, because it is more recent.
B) The ruling in the matn, because the mutun are the authoritative texts of the madhhab.
C) It depends on whether the ruling is explicitly corrected in the fatawa.
D) Both are given equal weight and the mufti chooses.

Key Takeaway
The ninth rule of Rasm al-Mufti establishes a clear hierarchy: explicit correction overrides implicit correction, but the mutun remain the primary source, with shuruh next, and fatawa only consulted when primary sources are silent or contain implicit correction.

Authoritative Mutun and Priority of Qawls in al-Khaniyya
Lists the authoritative mutun and provides a rule for prioritizing qawls from al-Khaniyya and similar works

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the seven authoritative mutun and apply the rule that the first qawl in al-Khaniyya has precedence when giving a fatwa.

Core Idea
In al-Khaniyya, the first qawl is preferred because Qadi Khan intentionally began with the most apparent and famous.

A mufti must know which texts are considered authoritative (mutun) and how to prioritize conflicting rulings within certain works. This passage clarifies the seven core mutun that transmit the madhhab from zahir al-riwaya and establishes a special rule for al-Khaniyya and Multaqa al-Abhath: the earlier qawl has precedence. Understanding this rule equips you to navigate these important fatwa collections correctly.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the seven authoritative mutun
Learn the list: al-Bidaya, Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, al-Nuqaya, al-Wiqaya, al-Kanz, al-Multaqa. These are the core texts transmitting the madhhab from zahir al-riwaya.
These are distinct from works like al-Ghurar and al-Tanwir which contain many later fatawa.
2
Distinguish mutun from fatwa collections
Understand that texts like al-Ghurar and al-Tanwir are not purely mutun because they include many issues from later fatawa. This affects their authority in the hierarchy.
The mufta bihi in later texts often relies on the mutun.
3
Grasp the priority rule for al-Khaniyya
In al-Khaniyya and Multaqa al-Abhath, the first qawl (سابق الأقوال) has precedence because the author Qadi Khan stated he began with the most apparent and famous opinion.
This is a specific rule, not a general one.
4
Apply the rule in fatwa practice
When a mufti consults al-Khaniyya for a case and finds multiple qawls, he should adopt the first one unless there is explicit evidence (dalil) supporting a later qawl or the later qawl comes from a stronger source (e.g., a matn).
The rule is a default; explicit correction overrides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم التصحيح الالتزامي ثم لا يخفى أن المراد بالمتون المتون المعتبرة كـ"البداية" و"مختصر القدوري"، و"المختار"، و"النقاية" ، و"الوقاية"، و"الكنز" ، و"الملتقى"، فإنها الموضوعة لنقل المذهب مما هو ظاهر الرواية، بخلاف متن "الغُرر" لملا خسرو، "التنوير" للتمرتاشي الغزي؛ فإن فيهما كثيرا من مسائل الفتاوى. وسابق الأقوال في الخانيه وملتقى الأبحـ ذو مزيه وفي سواهما اعتمد ما أخروا دليله لأنه هو العادة في الهدايه ونحوها الراجح المحرر الدرايه كذا إذا ما واحدا قد علّلوا له وتعليل سواه أهملوا أي إن أول الأقوال الواقعة في فتاوى الإمام قاضي خان له مزيّة على غيره في الرجحان؛ لأنه قال في أول الفتاوى: "وفيما كثرت فيه الأقاويل المتأخرين، اقتصرت على قول أو قولين، وقدمت ما من هو الأظهر. وافتتحت بما هو الأشهر؛ إجابة للطالبين وتيسيرا على الراغبين".
Division of implicit correction. Then it is not hidden that what is meant by 'mutun' are the reliable mutun, such as 'al-Bidaya', 'Mukhtasar al-Quduri', 'al-Mukhtar', 'al-Nuqaya', 'al-Wiqaya', 'al-Kanz', and 'al-Multaqa'. For they are composed to transmit the madhhab from what is zahir al-riwaya, unlike the matn of 'al-Ghurar' by Mulla Khusraw, and 'al-Tanwir' by al-Tamartashi al-Ghazzi; for in them there are many issues from the fatawa. And the earlier sayings in al-Khaniyya and Multaqa al-Abhath have precedence, and in other than them rely upon what they (the later ones) delayed its proof because it is the custom in al-Hidaya and similar works: the preferable, the verified, the proficient. Similarly, if they have given an illa for one, and the illa of other they ignored. That is, the first of the sayings occurring in the Fatawa of Imam Qadi Khan has precedence over others in being preferable; because he said at the beginning of the Fatawa: 'In those issues where the later scholars had many opinions, I restricted to one or two opinions, and I presented what is the most apparent. And I began with the most famous; in response to the seekers and to make it easy for those who desire.'
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the hierarchy of textual authorities, now focusing on which specific mutun are considered reliable (mu'tabar) and how to prioritize qawls within works like al-Khaniyya. The author lists seven authoritative mutun that transmit the madhhab from the zahir al-riwaya: al-Bidaya, Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, al-Nuqaya, al-Wiqaya, al-Kanz, and al-Multaqa. In contrast, other texts like al-Ghurar and al-Tanwir are not purely mutun because they contain many fatawa issues, mixing transmission of the madhhab with later opinions. Then a verse from the matn (the couplet) is quoted and explained: 'And the earlier sayings in al-Khaniyya and Multaqa al-Abhath have precedence, and in other than them rely upon what they (the later ones) delayed its proof because it is the custom in al-Hidaya and similar works: the preferable, the verified, the proficient. Similarly, if they have given an illa for one, and the illa of other they ignored.' The commentary clarifies that 'earlier sayings' refers to the first opinion mentioned in al-Khaniyya (Fatawa Qadi Khan). This is because Qadi Khan himself stated that he would present the most apparent (azhar) and most famous (ashhar) opinion first. Therefore, the first qawl in his work has a special status and is to be preferred over others within that same work. This rule aids muftis in selecting the correct opinion when multiple are recorded in al-Khaniyya or similar collections.

Key Terms
الخانية (al-Khaniyya): The Fatawa of Qadi Khan, a major work of Hanafi fatwas. It is a primary source for later rulings but is not considered a matn.
ملتقى الأبحاث (Multaqa al-Abhath): A Hanafi fiqh work by al-Halabi. It is mentioned as having the same rule of priority for earlier qawls.
سابق الأقوال (sabiq al-aqwal): The earlier statements or the first opinion mentioned in a work like al-Khaniyya. According to the text, it has precedence (maziyya) over later opinions in that same work.
متون معتبرة (mutun mu'tabara): Reliable or authoritative core texts that transmit the madhhab from the zahir al-riwaya. The text lists seven specific ones.

Key Takeaway
The seven mutun (al-Bidaya, Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, al-Nuqaya, al-Wiqaya, al-Kanz, al-Multaqa) are the authoritative core texts, and in al-Khaniyya the first qawl is given precedence unless overridden by explicit evidence.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Authoritative Texts and Tarjih Methods in Hanafi Fatwa
Discusses authoritative texts and methods of tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the authoritative texts (mutun mu'tabara) and apply the two main methods of tarjih — tas-hih iltizami (compiler commitment) and inferential tarjih (delayed proof and illah) — to determine the mufta bihi opinion in a given scenario.

Core Idea
Muftis rely on Zahir al-Riwayah texts and tarjih methods: compiler commitment, delayed proof, and illah.

When faced with multiple opinions in the Hanafi school, how does a mufti choose? This passage from Rasm al-Mufti explains two key areas: which texts are authoritative and how to prefer one qawl over another. You will learn the core mutun and the principles of tas-hih iltizami and inferential tarjih, enabling you to navigate Hanafi fatwa literature with confidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the authoritative mutun
Locate the list of core texts that transmit the madhhab from Zahir al-Riwayah: al-Bidayah, Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, al-Niqayah, al-Wiqayah, al-Kanz, and al-Multaqa. Note that later texts like al-Ghurar and al-Tanwir are not primary.
These six texts form the reliable foundation for fatwa extraction.
2
Understand the status of fatwa collections
Recognize that collections like Fatawa Qadi Khan (al-Khaniyyah) and Multaqa al-Abhar are compilations of preferred opinions from the mashayikh, not contradictory to the madhhab.
Their introductions often state the compiler's method, which is a form of tas-hih iltizami.
3
Apply tas-hih iltizami
When a compiler explicitly states that he presents the most apparent or relied-upon opinion (e.g., Qadi Khan: 'I confined myself to one or two statements and presented what is more apparent'), the mufti follows that commitment.
This is an explicit tarjih method, stronger than inferential cues.
4
Apply inferential tarjih in detailed books
In books that discuss proofs (e.g., al-Hidayah, its commentaries), the custom is to delay the Imam's statement and give his proof last. This delay indicates preponderance. Additionally, if a qawl is accompanied by an illah, it is preponderant over one without.
If both methods conflict, explicit commitment (iltizami) may override, but the passage does not specify a hierarchy.
5
Integrate both methods
Determine the mufta bihi by first checking if the compiler has an explicit commitment; if not, apply the inferential cues: delayed proof and illah. Use the principle 'work is upon the statement of Abu Hanifah' as default.
This integration is essential for accurate fatwa derivation.

خالفه
قال شيخ الإسلام العلامة ابن الشلبي في فتاواه: "الأصل أن العمل على قول أبي حنيفة، ولذا ترجح المشايخ دليله في الأغلب على دليل من أصحابه، ويجيبون عما استدل به مخالفه، وهذا أمارة العمل من بقوله، وإن لم يصرحوا بالفتوى عليه؛ إذ الترجيح كصريح التصحيح". وفي آخر المستصفى للإمام النسفي إذا ذكر في المسألة ثلاثة أقوال، فالراجح هو الأول أو الأخير لا الوسط. قلت وينبغي تقييده بما إذا لم تعلم عادة صاحب ذلك الكتاب، و لم يذكر الأدلة. أما إذا علمت كما مر عن "الخانية" و"الملتقى" فتتبع، وأما إذا ذكرت الأدلة، فالمرجح الأخير كما قلنا.
وكذا لو ذكروا قولين مثلا وعللوا لأحدها كان ترجيحا له على غير المعلل، كما أفاده الخير الرملي في كتاب الغصب من فتاواه "الخيرية". ونظيره ما في "التحرير" وشرحه في فصل الترجيح في المتعارضين: "أن الحكم الذي تعرض فيه للعلة يترجح على الحكم الذي لم يتعرض فيه لها؛ لأن ذكر علته يدل على الاهتمام به والحث عليه".
Classification of obligatory correction. Commentary on 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti': I saw in some books of later scholars, quoting from 'Idah al-Istidlal 'ala Ibtal al-Istibdal' by Qadi al-Qudat Shams al-Din al-Hariri, one of the commentators of al-Hidayah: 'That Sadr al-Din Sulayman said: These fatwas are the choices of the mashayikh, so they do not contradict the books of the madhhab. He said: And others of our mashayikh used to say likewise, and I say that.' [Authoritative texts] Then it is not hidden that by 'mutun' are meant the authoritative texts such as al-Bidayah, Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, al-Niqayah, al-Wiqayah, al-Kanz, and al-Multaqa. For they are composed to transmit the madhhab from what is Zahir al-Riwayah, unlike the matn of al-Ghurar by Mulla Khusraw and al-Tanwir by al-Tamartashi al-Ghazzi, for in them there are many issues from fatwas. And the earlier statements in al-Khaniyyah and Multaqa al-Abhar have a merit, and in others rely on what they delayed its proof. As is the custom in al-Hidayah and similar, the rajih (preponderant) is the one whose proof is mentioned last, and likewise if they provide an illah for one and ignore the illah of the other. Classification of obligatory correction: i.e., the first statements occurring in the fatwas of Imam Qadi Khan have a merit over others in preponderance because he said at the beginning of his fatwas: 'And in what has many statements from the later scholars, I confined myself to one or two statements, and I presented what is more apparent. And I began with what is more famous, in answer to the seekers and to facilitate for the desirous.' Likewise, the author of Multaqa al-Abhar committed to presenting the relied-upon statement. Other than these two are the books that mention statements with their proofs, such as al-Hidayah and its commentaries, and the commentaries of al-Kanz, and Kafi al-Nasafi, and al-Bada'i', and other detailed books. The custom in them when narrating the statements is that they delay the statement of the Imam, then mention the proof of each statement, then mention the proof of the Imam, including the answer to what others used as evidence. And this is a preponderance for him unless they explicitly state the preponderance of another. Shaykh al-Islam al-Allamah Ibn al-Shilbi said in his fatwas: 'The principle is that action is upon the statement of Abu Hanifah, and thus the mashayikh give preponderance to his proof in most cases over the proof of his companions, and they answer what the opponent used as evidence. And this is an indication of acting by his statement, even if they do not explicitly give fatwa upon it, because preponderance is like explicit correction.' And in the end of al-Mustasfa by Imam al-Nasafi: If three statements are mentioned in an issue, the rajih is the first or the last, not the middle. I say: It should be restricted to when the custom of the book's author is not known, and proofs are not mentioned. As for when it is known, as mentioned about al-Khaniyyah and al-Multaqa, then one follows that; and if proofs are mentioned, then the last is preponderant as we said. Likewise, if they mention two statements, for example, and provide an illah for one, that is a preponderance for it over the one without illah, as al-Khayr al-Ramli expressed in the book of Ghasb from his fatwas 'al-Khayriyyah'. And similar is what is in al-Tahrir and its commentary in the chapter of tarjih in conflicting evidences: 'That the ruling in which an illah is mentioned is preponderant over the ruling in which it is not mentioned, because mentioning its illah indicates attention to it and urging upon it.'
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage from Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti deals with the crucial topic of tarjih (preferring one position over another) in Hanafi fatwa. The author begins by quoting a principle from earlier scholars that fatwa collections (like Fatawa Qadi Khan) represent the choices of the mashayikh and do not contradict the established books of the madhhab. He then lists the authoritative texts (al-mutun al-mu'tabara) that transmit the madhhab from what is known as Zahir al-Riwayah: al-Bidayah, Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, al-Niqayah, al-Wiqayah, al-Kanz, and al-Multaqa. These are distinguished from texts like al-Ghurar and al-Tanwir which include many fatwa-based issues. The following poetic lines summarize the rule: the earlier statements in al-Khaniyyah (Fatawa Qadi Khan) and Multaqa al-Abhar have merit, and in other books one relies on the statement whose proof is delayed (i.e., usually the last mentioned). This is the custom in books like al-Hidayah, where the Imam's proof is mentioned last, indicating its preponderance. The author further explains that the author of al-Khaniyyah explicitly states that he presents the most apparent and famous position, while the author of Multaqa al-Abhar commits to presenting the relied-upon position. In other comprehensive books like al-Hidayah and its commentaries, the practice is to delay the Imam's statement (Abu Hanifah), then give the proofs of each, and then give the Imam's proof which includes a refutation of the others—this constitutes a preponderance for Abu Hanifah unless they explicitly prefer another. Ibn al-Shilbi affirms that this practice of giving the Imam's proof and answering opponents is a clear indication of acting on his statement, even without explicit fatwa. The passage ends with two additional points from al-Nasafi and al-Khayr al-Ramli: when three statements are given, the first or last is preponderant (with a qualification from the commentator), and if a ruling is accompanied by an illah (legal cause), it is preponderant over one without an illah. This entire discussion is foundational for a mufti to correctly navigate differing opinions within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
متون معتبرة (mutun mu'tabara): Authoritative texts that transmit the Hanafi madhhab from Zahir al-Riwayah, such as al-Bidayah, Mukhtasar al-Quduri, etc.
تصحيح التزامي (tashih iltizami): Obligatory correction: the method of preferring one qawl over another based on the compiler's commitment (e.g., Qadi Khan's explicit method).
ترجيح (tarjih): The act of preferring one legal opinion over another based on evidence or compiler practice.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi mufti must identify authoritative texts and apply tas-hih iltizami or inferential tarjih (delayed proof and illah) to determine the mufta bihi opinion.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Determining the Mu'tamad from Book Structure and 'Illa
Classifying books by their method of presenting authoritative opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the signs of tarjīḥ (preference) in Hanafi fiqh books and apply the principles that the Imam's opinion is default mu'tamad unless an explicit preference or 'illa indicates otherwise.

Core Idea
In Hanafi books presenting multiple opinions, the author's arrangement and provision of 'illa indicate which opinion is mu'tamad.

This lesson teaches you how to read a Hanafi fiqh book like al-Hidayah to find the authoritative (mu'tamad) ruling. Not all books are the same: some commit to giving only the relied-upon opinion (like Multaqa al-Abhar), while others present multiple views with evidence. The key skill is recognizing the author's signals of tarjīḥ — delaying the Imam's opinion, providing his proof with responses, or attaching a legal reason ('illa) to one view. Mastering this allows you to correctly derive the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Classify the book type
First, determine whether the book follows a 'committed correction' method (like al-Multaqa) where only the mu'tamad is stated, or the 'detailed' method (like al-Hidayah) where multiple opinions and their proofs are given.
If the book is committed, the answer is explicit. If detailed, proceed to the next steps.
2
Locate the Imam's opinion and its position
In detailed books, the custom is to mention the Imam's (Abu Hanifa's) opinion last among the opinions and then provide its proof, often with a rebuttal of opposing proofs.
This ordering itself is a form of tarjīḥ for the Imam's view.
3
Check for explicit tarjīḥ or 'illa
If the author explicitly states preference for another opinion, that overrides the default. Also, if one opinion is accompanied by a legal reason ('illa), that opinion is preferred as the mu'tamad.
The presence of an 'illa shows the author's care and emphasis, thus indicating preference.
4
Apply the default principle when signals are absent
If the book's custom is unknown and no proofs are given, apply the rule from al-Mustasfā: among three opinions, the first or last is preponderant (not the middle). If proofs are given, the last opinion is preponderant.
This is a fallback; always look for explicit signals first.
5
Synthesize the mu'tamad
Combine all signals: the default preference for Imam Abu Hanifa, the ordering, explicit tarjīḥ statements, and the presence of 'illa to determine the authoritative ruling for fatwa.
The goal is to identify the muftā bihī (operative opinion) using the book's internal methodology.

Source Text

تقسيم التصحيح الالتزامي
صاحب "ملتقى الأبحر" التزم تقديم القول المعتمد. وما عداهما الكتب التي تذكر فيها الأقوال بأدلتها كـ"الهداية" من وشروحها. وشروح "الكنز"، و"كافي النسفي"، و"البدائع" وغيرها من الكتب المبسوطة، فقد جرت العادة فيها عند حكاية الأقوال أنهم يؤخرون قول الإمام، ثم يذكرون دليل كل قول، ثم يذكرون دليل الإمام متضمنا للجواب عما استدل به غيره. وهذا ترجيح له إلا أن ينصوا على ترجيح غيره.
خالفه
قال شيخ الإسلام العلامة ابن الشلبي في فتاواه: "الأصل أن العمل على قول أبي حنيفة، ولذا ترجح المشايخ دليله في الأغلب على دليل من أصحابه، ويجيبون عما استدل به مخالفه، وهذا أمارة العمل من بقوله، وإن لم يصرحوا بالفتوى عليه؛ إذ الترجيح كصريح التصحيح". وفي آخر المستصفى للإمام النسفي إذا ذكر في المسألة ثلاثة أقوال، فالراجح هو الأول أو الأخير لا الوسط. قلت وينبغي تقييده بما إذا لم تعلم عادة صاحب ذلك الكتاب، و لم يذكر الأدلة. أما إذا علمت كما مر عن "الخانية" و"الملتقى" فتتبع، وأما إذا ذكرت الأدلة، فالمرجح الأخير كما قلنا.
وكذا لو ذكروا قولين مثلا وعللوا لأحدهما كان ترجيحا له على غير المعلل، كما أفاده الخير الرملي في كتاب الغصب من فتاواه "الخيرية". ونظيره ما في " "التحرير" وشرحه في فصل الترجيح في المتعارضين: "أن الحكم الذي تعرض فيه للعلة يترجح على الحكم الذي لم يتعرض فيه
لها؛ لأن ذكر علته يدل على الاهتمام به والحث عليه".
Division of binding correction
The author of Multaqa al-Abhar committed to presenting the relied-upon opinion. Other books are those that mention opinions with their proofs, like al-Hidayah and its commentaries, and commentaries of al-Kanz, and Kafi al-Nasafi, and al-Bada'i‘ and other detailed books. Their custom when relating opinions is to delay the Imam's opinion, then mention the proof of each opinion, then mention the Imam's proof including a response to what others used as proof. This is a preference (tarjih) for his opinion unless they explicitly state preference for another.
He disagreed: Shaykh al-Islam al-Allamah Ibn al-Shalabi said in his Fatawa: "The principle is that action is upon the saying of Abu Hanifa. Therefore, the mashayikh generally prefer his proof over the proof of his companions, and they respond to what his opponent used as proof. This indicates acting upon his saying, even if they do not explicitly give fatwa upon it, because preference is like explicit correction." At the end of al-Mustasfa by Imam al-Nasafi: If three opinions are mentioned in a matter, the preponderant is the first or the last, not the middle. I say: It should be restricted to when the custom of the book's author is unknown and proofs are not mentioned. If known, as mentioned about al-Khaniyya and al-Multaqa, then it is followed. If proofs are mentioned, the preponderant is the last, as we said.
Similarly, if they mention two opinions and provide a reason for one, that is a tarjih for the reasoned one over the unreasoned, as al-Khayr al-Ramli stated in his Fatawa 'al-Khayriyya' in the chapter of ghasp. Comparable to what is in al-Tahrir and its commentary in the chapter of tarjih of conflicting evidences: "A ruling that is accompanied by its 'illa is preferred over a ruling that is not accompanied by its 'illa, because mentioning its 'illa indicates care for it and emphasis upon it."
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage, from a late Hanafi work on the methodology of fatwa, discusses how to determine the authoritative (mu'tamad) opinion from different types of fiqh books. The author first distinguishes two categories of books: those like Multaqa al-Abhar that commit to presenting only the relied-upon opinion, and those that present multiple opinions with their evidence. In the latter category (e.g., al-Hidayah, al-Kanz, al-Bada'i'), the standard practice is to defer the Imam's (Abu Hanifa's) opinion and then give his proof, often including a refutation of the opposing evidence. This itself is a form of tarjih (preference) unless they explicitly prefer another. Ibn al-Shalabi affirms the principle that action is on Abu Hanifa's saying, and the mashayikh's defense of his proof is a sign of that. The passage also notes a rule from al-Mustasfa: when three opinions are cited, the preferred is the first or last, not the middle, but this is qualified by the author to apply only when the book's method is unknown and no evidence is given. Finally, the author notes that when scholars provide an 'illa (legal reason) for one opinion over another, that constitutes tarjih, as citing the reason shows they prioritize it.

Key Terms
تصحيح التزامي (tashīh iltizāmī): A committed method of correction where the author pledges to present only the mu'tamad (relied-upon) opinion.
ترجيح (tarjīh): The act of preferring one opinion over another, either explicitly or implicitly through arrangement and evidence presentation.
علة (ʿilla): A legal reason or effective cause that justifies a ruling; mentioned to indicate preference.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does it mean when a book like al-Hidayah mentions Abu Hanifa's opinion last along with his proof?
A) That is a sign of tarjih for his opinion.
B) That is just a random order with no significance.
C) That means the author disagrees with Abu Hanifa.
D) That means the companion's opinion is stronger.

Key Takeaway
Always look at the author's arrangement, the presence of rebuttals, and the mention of 'illa to determine which opinion is mu'tamad, with the default being the saying of Abu Hanifa.

The Signs for Fatwa and the Hierarchy of Terms
Listing and ranking the signs for fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the hierarchy of terms used in Hanafi fatwa literature (sahih, asahh, al-fatwa, etc.) and apply the rule to determine the mu'tamad opinion.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fatwa, 'al-fatwa' is the strongest sign; 'asahh' is stronger than 'sahih'—but a minority view reverses this.

This passage from Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti introduces the technical vocabulary that Hanafi jurists use to signal the authoritative opinion for fatwa. These signs are not all equal; they have a distinct hierarchy. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for any mufti or student of Hanafi fiqh to correctly weigh conflicting opinions and issue sound fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for the mu'tamad
The passage states: wherever two opinions exist and one is explicitly 'corrected' (suhhiha), that is the mu'tamad. Identify the phrases that indicate this correction.
These phrases include: 'al-fatwa alayhi', 'al-ashbah', 'al-azhar', 'al-mukhtar', 'al-awjah', 'al-sahih', etc.
2
List the complete set of signs for fatwa
The passage quotes al-Fatawa al-Khayriyya and al-Mudmarat, listing over a dozen signs. Enumerate them and note that they are not all of equal strength.
Examples: 'wa alayhi al-fatwa', 'bihi yufta', 'huwa al-sahih', 'huwa al-asahh', etc.
3
Establish the hierarchy of strength
The passage explicitly ranks some signs: 'al-fatwa' is strongest; 'bihi yufti' is stronger than 'al-fatwa alayhi'; 'asahh' is stronger than 'sahih' (majority view); 'ahwat' is stronger than 'ihtiyat'.
This hierarchy is crucial when two signs appear on different opinions.
4
Analyze the ikhtilaf on sahih vs asahh
The passage presents a dissenting view from Sharh al-Munyah that 'sahih' should be preferred when two imams disagree. Examine the reasoning on both sides.
The majority follows the literal text 'wa al-asahh akad min al-sahih', but the minority argues from the practice of the mashayikh.
5
Apply the hierarchy to a fatwa scenario
Given two opinions with different signs, decide which is mufta bihi using the hierarchy. If signs are equal in strength, consider other factors like the authority of the imam.
The passage leaves the ikhtilaf unresolved, so the mufti must be aware of both views.

Is 'sahih' stronger or 'asahh'?
Explanation of 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti
Wherever two opinions are found and one has been corrected (suhhiha), then that is the mu'tamad, like: al-fatwa alayhi, al-ashbah, al-azhar, al-mukhtar, and al-awjah, or al-sahih. And al-asahh is stronger than it, and it is said: the opposite. The mu'akkad likewise: bihi yufta, al-fatwa, and those two are stronger than all those signs [for fatwa].
He said in the end of al-Fatawa al-Khayriyya and the beginning of al-Mudmarat: "As for the signs for fatwa, they are: 'wa alayhi al-fatwa', 'bihi yufta', 'bihi na'khudh', 'alayhi al-i'timad', 'alayhi amal al-yawm', 'alayhi amal al-umma', 'huwa al-sahih', 'huwa al-asahh', 'al-azhar', 'huwa al-mukhtar fi zamanina', 'fatwa mashayikhina', 'huwa al-ashbah', 'huwa al-awjah', and other expressions mentioned in the text of this book in their places." And some of these expressions are stronger than others. The expression 'al-fatwa' is stronger than 'sahih', 'asahh', 'ashbah', etc. And 'bihi yufti' is stronger than 'al-fatwa alayhi'. And 'asahh' is stronger than 'sahih'. And 'ahwat' is stronger than 'ihtiyat'.
Is 'sahih' stronger or 'asahh'?
But in Sharh al-Munyah in the discussion of touching the mushaf, what we have taken from the mashayikh is that when two reliable imams conflict in correction, one saying 'al-sahih is such' and another saying 'al-asahh is such', then taking the one who said 'al-sahih' is more appropriate than taking the one who said 'al-asahh', because 'al-sahih'...
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage from Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti is a detailed exposition of the technical terms used by Hanafi scholars to indicate the authoritative (mu'tamad) position for fatwa. It begins with a general rule: whenever two opinions exist and one is explicitly 'corrected' (suhhiha), that is the mu'tamad. The author then lists the common terms of tarjih: 'al-fatwa alayhi', 'al-ashbah', 'al-azhar', 'al-mukhtar', 'al-awjah', 'al-sahih', etc. A key statement is that 'asahh' is stronger than 'sahih', but there is also a contrary view. The passage then quotes al-Fatawa al-Khayriyya and al-Mudmarat which enumerate the full set of signs for fatwa. These signs have a hierarchy: 'al-fatwa' is the strongest, stronger than 'sahih', 'asahh', etc. Then 'bihi yufti' is stronger than 'al-fatwa alayhi'. And importantly, 'asahh' is stronger than 'sahih' according to the majority. However, a dissenting view from Sharh al-Munyah is cited: when two imams disagree, one saying 'sahih' and the other 'asahh', the opinion labeled 'sahih' should be taken, implying 'sahih' is stronger. The passage ends mid-sentence explaining why, but the student should note the ikhtilaf. The practical outcome is that these terms are not all equal, and a mufti must weigh them carefully. In the Hanafi school, the strongest terms for fatwa are 'al-fatwa' and 'bihi yufta'.

Key Terms
الصحيح (al-ṣaḥīḥ): A term used by Hanafi jurists to indicate that an opinion is correct and reliable for fatwa, but less strong than 'asahh' according to many.
الأصح (al-aṣaḥḥ): A term indicating the 'more correct' opinion, considered stronger than 'sahih' by the majority of Hanafi scholars.
العلامات للإفتاء (al-ʿalāmāt lil-iftāʾ): The set of phrases used by Hanafi authors to signal the relied-upon (mu'tamad) opinion for issuing fatwas.
الفتوى (al-fatwā): In this context, a term used as a sign for fatwa, considered the strongest indicator of the mu'tamad opinion.

C) 'Al-fatwa' is weaker than 'sahih'.
D) 'Bihi yufti' is weaker than 'al-fatwa alayhi'.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa literature uses a hierarchy of signs, with 'al-fatwa' as the strongest and 'asahh' generally stronger than 'sahih', though a recognized ikhtilaf exists.

Hierarchy of Fatwa Indication Terms: Sahih vs. Asahh
Clarifying which term (as-Sahih or al-Asahh) is stronger in fatwa texts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relative strength of fatwa indication terms (sahih, asahh, fatwa alayhi, etc.) and apply the practical rule from Risala Adab al-Muftin to determine when a mufti may depart from a ruling.

Core Idea
As-Sahih restricts the mufti; al-Asahh allows choice — the wording determines the scope of fatwa.

In Hanafi fatwa literature, terms like 'sahih', 'asahh', and 'al-fatwa alayhi' are not equally binding. This passage from Uqud Rasm al-Mufti clarifies the hierarchy and provides a practical rule that every mufti must internalise. Understanding this hierarchy prevents incorrect fatwas and ensures that the mufti's choice is grounded in the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of fatwa indicators
Read the opening citation from Al-Fatawa al-Khairiyya and Al-Mudmarat. Note the expressions: 'al-fatwa alayhi', 'bihi yufta', 'bihi na'khudh', 'alayhi al-i'timad', 'alayhi al-amal al-yawm', 'alayhi amal al-ummah', 'huwa al-sahih', 'huwa al-asahh', 'al-adhar', 'al-mukhtar', 'fatwa mashayikhina', 'al-ashbah', 'al-awjah'.
This list is not exhaustive but covers the most common indicators.
2
Note the general hierarchy
The author states that some terms are stronger than others: 'al-fatwa' (i.e., 'al-fatwa alayhi') is stronger than 'sahih', 'asahh', 'ashbah', etc.; 'bihi yufta' is stronger than 'al-fatwa alayhi'; 'asahh' is stronger than 'sahih'; 'ahwat' is stronger than 'ihtiyat'.
This is the initial general statement, but it is later refined.
3
Understand the apparent contradiction
The passage from Sharh al-Munya suggests the opposite: when two imams differ, one saying 'sahih' and the other 'asahh', preference is given to the one saying 'sahih' because its opposite is false. This seems to contradict the general statement that 'asahh' is stronger.
Sharh al-Munya's reasoning: 'sahih' implies the opposite is false, while 'asahh' concedes the other view may also be sahih.
4
Analyse the linguistic implication
Al-Bazdawi's gloss explains: 'sahih' implies that anything else is not sahih; 'asahh' implies that the opposite may still be sahih. This supports the view that 'sahih' is more restrictive. However, it is qualified by the fact that the opposite of 'asahh' is often a rare narration (shadhdh).
Thus, the strength depends on context: in a direct conflict of two imams, 'sahih' takes precedence; in general usage, 'asahh' may be considered stronger by later scholars.
5
Apply the practical rule from Risala Adab al-Muftin
The final rule: if a ruling is annotated with 'asahh' (or similar weaker terms like 'awla', 'arfaq'), the mufti may follow it or its opposite. If annotated with 'sahih', 'ma'khudh bihi', 'bihi yufta', or 'alayhi al-fatwa', the mufti must not depart from it, unless there is a conflict between equally authoritative books (e.g., 'sahih' in Al-Hidayah vs. 'sahih' in Al-Kafi holding the opposite), in which case he chooses the stronger.
This is the operative rule for fatwa today.

Source Text
